<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3><br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNopinion1.1" href="#FRopinion1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Alito</small></span> distinguishes <em>Stevens</em> on several grounds that seem to us ill founded.  He suggests, <em>post</em>, at 10 (opinion concurring in judgment), that <em>Stevens </em>did not apply strict scrutiny.  If that is so (and we doubt it), it would make this an <em>a fortiori </em>case.  He says, <em>post</em>, at 9, 10, that the California Act punishes the sale or rental rather than the "creation" or "possession" of violent depictions.  That distinction appears nowhere in <em>Stevens</em> itself, and for good reason: It would make permissible the prohibition of printing or selling books--though not the writing of them.  Whether government regulation applies to creating, distributing, or consuming speech makes no difference.  And finally, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Alito </small></span>points out, <em>post</em>, at 10, that <em>Stevens </em>"left open the possibility that a more narrowly drawn statute" would be constitutional.  True, but entirely irrelevant.  <em>Stevens </em>said, 559 U.&nbsp;S., at ___ (slip op., at 19), that the "crush-video" statute at issue there might pass muster if it were limited to videos of acts of animal cruelty that violated the law where the acts were performed.  There is no contention that any of the virtual characters depicted in the imaginative videos at issue here are criminally liable. </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNopinion1.2" href="#FRopinion1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The statute in <em>Ginsberg</em> restricted the sale of certain depictions of "nudity, sexual conduct, sexual excitement, or sado-masochistic abuse," that were "&nbsp;'[h]armful to minors.'&nbsp;"  A depiction was harmful to minors if it:</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(i) predominantly appeals to the prurient, shameful or morbid interests of minors, and </p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(ii) is patently offensive to prevailing standards in the adult community as a whole with respect to what is suitable material for minors, and</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"(iii) is utterly without redeeming social importance for minors."  390 U.&nbsp;S., at 646 (Appendix A to opinion of the Court) (quoting N.&nbsp;Y. Penal Law 484-h(1)(f)).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNopinion1.3" href="#FRopinion1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Thomas</small></span> ignores the holding of <em>Erznoznik</em>, and<em> </em>denies that persons under 18 have any constitutional right to speak or be spoken to without their parents' consent.  He cites no case, state or federal, supporting this view, and to our knowledge there is none.  Most of his dissent is devoted to the proposition that parents have traditionally had the power to control what their children hear and say.  This is true enough.  And it perhaps follows from this that the state has the power to <em>enforce</em> parental prohibitions--to require, for example, that the promoters of a rock concert exclude those minors whose parents have advised the promoters that their children are forbidden to attend.  But it does not follow that the state has the power to prevent children from hearing or saying anything <em>without their parents' prior consent</em>.  The latter would mean, for example, that it could be made criminal to admit persons under 18 to a political rally without their parents' prior written consent--even a political rally in support of laws against corporal punishment of children, or laws in favor of greater rights for minors.  And what is good for First Amendment rights of speech must be good for First Amendment rights of religion as well: It could be made criminal to admit a person under 18 to church, or to give a person under 18 a religious tract, without his parents' prior consent.  Our point is not, as <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Thomas</small></span> believes, <em>post</em>, at 16, n.&nbsp;2, merely that such laws are "undesirable."  They are obviously an infringement upon the religious freedom of young people and those who wish to proselytize young people.  Such laws do not enforce <em>parental</em> authority over children's speech and religion; they impose <em>governmental</em> authority, subject only to a parental veto.  In the absence of any precedent for state control, uninvited by the parents, over a child's speech and religion (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Thomas</small></span> cites none), and in the absence of any justification for such control that would satisfy strict scrutiny, those laws must be unconstitutional.  This argument is not, as <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Thomas</small></span> asserts, "circular," <em>ibid.</em>  It is the absence of any historical warrant or compelling justification for such restrictions, not our <em>ipse dixit</em>, that renders them invalid. </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNopinion1.4" href="#FRopinion1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Alito </small></span>accuses us of pronouncing that playing violent video games "is not different in 'kind'&nbsp;" from reading violent literature.  <em>Post</em>, at 2.  Well of course it is different in kind, but not in a way that causes the provision and viewing of violent video games, unlike the provision and reading of books, not to be expressive activity and hence not to enjoy First Amendment protection.  Reading Dante is unquestionably more cultured and intellectually edifying than playing Mortal Kombat.  But these cultural and intellectual differences are not <em>constitutional</em> ones.<em>  </em>Crudely violent video games, tawdry TV shows, and cheap novels and magazines are no less forms of speech than The Divine Comedy, and restrictions upon them must survive strict scrutiny--a question to which we devote our attention in Part III, <em>infra.</em>  Even if we can see in them "nothing of any possible value to society . . . , they are as much entitled to the protection of free speech as the best of literature."  <em>Winters </em>v.<em> New York</em>, 333 U.&nbsp;S. 507, 510 (1948). </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNopinion1.5" href="#FRopinion1.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The crusade against comic books was led by a psychiatrist, Frederic Wertham, who told the Senate Judiciary Committee that "as long as the crime comic books industry exists in its present forms there are no secure homes."  Juvenile Delinquency (Comic Books): Hearings before the Subcommittee to Investigate Juvenile Delinquency, 83d Cong., 2d Sess., 84 (1954).  Wertham's objections extended even to Superman<em> </em>comics, which he described as "particularly injurious to the ethical development of children."  <em>Id.</em>, at 86.  Wertham's crusade did convince the New York Legislature to pass a ban on the sale of certain comic books to minors, but it was vetoed by Governor Thomas Dewey on the ground that it was unconstitutional given our opinion in <em>Winters, supra.</em>  See <em>People</em> v. <em>Bookcase, Inc.</em>, 14 N.&nbsp;Y. 2d 409, 412-413, 201 N. E. 2d 14, 15-16 (1964).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNopinion1.6" href="#FRopinion1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See <em>Video Software Dealers Assn.</em> v.<em> Schwarzenegger,</em> 556 F.&nbsp;3d 950, 963-964 (CA9 2009); <em>Interactive Digital Software Assn.</em> v. <em>St.&nbsp;Louis County, </em>329 F.&nbsp;3d 954 (CA8 2003); <em>American Amusement Machine Assn.</em> v. <em>Kendrick</em>, 244 F.&nbsp;3d 572, 578-579 (CA7 2001); <em>Entertainment Software Assn. </em>v. <em>Foti</em>, 451 F.&nbsp;Supp. 2d 823, 832-833 (MD La. 2006); <em>Entertainment Software Assn.</em> v. <em>Hatch</em>, 443 F. Supp. 2d 1065, 1070 (Minn. 2006), aff 'd, 519 F.&nbsp;3d 768 (CA8 2008); <em>Entertainment Software Assn.</em> v. <em>Granholm</em>, 426 F.&nbsp;Supp. 2d 646, 653 (ED Mich. 2006); <em>Entertainment Software Assn.</em> v. <em>Blagojevich</em>, 404 F.&nbsp;Supp. 2d 1051, 1063 (ND Ill. 2005), aff 'd, 469 F.&nbsp;3d 641 (CA7 2006).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNopinion1.7" href="#FRopinion1.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;One study, for example, found that children who had just finished playing violent video games were more likely to fill in the blank letter in "explo_e" with a "d" (so that it reads "explode") than with an "r" ("explore").  App. 496, 506 (internal quotation marks omitted).  The prevention of this phenomenon, which might have been anticipated with common sense, is not a compelling state interest.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 8" name="FNopinion1.8" href="#FRopinion1.8">8</a></strong><br><p><span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Alito</small></span> is mistaken in thinking that we fail to take account of "new and rapidly evolving technology," <em>post</em>, at 1.  The studies in question pertain to that new and rapidly evolving technology, and fail to show, with the degree of certitude that strict scrutiny requires, that this subject-matter restriction on speech is justified.  Nor is <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Alito </small></span>correct in attributing to us the view that "violent video games really present no serious problem."  <em>Post</em>, at 2.  Perhaps they do present a problem, and perhaps none of us would allow our own children to play them.  But there are all sorts of "problems"--some of them surely more serious than this one--that cannot be addressed by governmental restriction of free expression: for example, the problem of encouraging anti-Semitism (<em>National Socialist Party of America</em> v. <em>Skokie</em>, 432 U.&nbsp;S. 43 (1977) <em>(per curiam)</em>), the problem of spreading a political philosophy hostile to the Constitution (<em>Noto</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 367 U.&nbsp;S. 290 (1961)), or the problem of encouraging disrespect for the Nation's flag (<em>Texas</em> v. <em>Johnson</em>, 491 U.&nbsp;S. 397 (1989)).</p>
<p>

<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Justice Breyer</small></span> would hold that California has satisfied strict scrutiny based upon his own research into the issue of the harmfulness of violent video games.  See <em>post</em>, at 20-35 (Appendixes to dissenting opinion) (listing competing academic articles discussing the harmfulness <em>vel non</em> of violent video games).  The vast preponderance of this research is outside the record--and in any event we do not see how it could lead to <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Breyer</small></span>'s conclusion, since he admits he cannot say whether the studies on his side are right or wrong.  <em>Post</em>, at 15.  Similarly, <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Alito</small></span> says he is not "sure" whether there are any constitutionally dispositive differences between video games and other media.  <em>Post</em>, at 2.  If that is so, then strict scrutiny plainly has not been satisfied.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 9" name="FNopinion1.9" href="#FRopinion1.9">9</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Breyer </small></span>concludes that the remaining gap is compelling because, according to the FTC's report, some "20% of those under 17 are still able to buy M-rated games."  <em>Post</em>, at 18 (citing FTC Report 28).  But some gap in compliance is unavoidable.  The sale of alcohol to minors, for example, has long been illegal, but a 2005 study suggests that about 18% of retailers still sell alcohol to those under the drinking age.  Brief for State of Rhode Island et&nbsp;al<em>.</em> as <em>Amici Curiae</em> 18.  Even if the sale of violent video games to minors could be deterred further by increasing regulation, the government does not have a compelling interest in each marginal percentage point by which its goals are advanced.</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3><br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNconcurrence1.1" href="#FRconcurrence1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;It is well established that a judgment may be affirmed on an alternative ground that was properly raised but not addressed by the lower court.  <em>Washington</em> v. <em>Confederated Bands and Tribes of Yakima Nation</em>, 439 U.&nbsp;S. 463, 478, n.&nbsp;20 (1979).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNconcurrence1.2" href="#FRconcurrence1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Under the California law, a game that meets the threshold requirement set out in text also qualifies as "violent" if it "[e]nables the player to virtually inflict serious injury upon images of human beings or characters with substantially human characteristics in a manner which is especially heinous, cruel, or depraved in that it involves torture or serious physical abuse to the victim."  1746(d)(1)(B).  In the Court of Appeals, California conceded that this alternative definition is unconstitutional, 556 F.&nbsp;3d 950, 954, n.&nbsp;5 (CA9 2009), and therefore only the requirements set out in text are now before us.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNconcurrence1.3" href="#FRconcurrence1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The provision of New York law under which the petitioner was convicted in <em>Ginsberg</em> was framed with similar specificity.  This provision applied to depictions of "nudity" and "sexual conduct," and both those terms were specifically and unambiguously defined.  See 390 U.&nbsp;S., at 645-647 (Appendix A to opinion of the Court).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNconcurrence1.4" href="#FRconcurrence1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The California law does not define the term "maiming," nor has the State cited any decisions from its courts that define the term in this context.  Accordingly, I take the term to have its ordinary meaning, which includes the infliction of any serious wound, see Webster's Third New International Dictionary 1362 (2002) (hereinafter Webster's).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNconcurrence1.5" href="#FRconcurrence1.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;At oral argument, California also proposed that the term "minors" could be interpreted as referring to the "typical age group of minors" who play video games.  Tr. of Oral Arg. 11.  But nothing in the law's text supports such a limitation.  Nor has California cited any decisions indicating that its courts would restrict the law in this way.  And there is nothing in the record indicating what this age group might be.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNconcurrence1.6" href="#FRconcurrence1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;A 2004 Federal Trade Commission Report showed that 69 percent of unaccompanied children ages 13 to 16 were able to buy M-rated games and that 56 percent of 13-year-olds were able to buy an M-rated game.  Marketing Violent Entertainment to Children: A Fourth Follow-Up Review of Industry Practices in the Motion Picture, Music Recording &amp; Electronic Game Industries 26-28 (July 2004), http://www.ftc.gov/os/ 2004/07/040708kidsviolencerpt.pdf (all Internet materials as visited June 24, 2011, and available in Clerk of Court's case file).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNconcurrence1.7" href="#FRconcurrence1.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Chayka, Visual Games: Photorealism in Crisis, Kill Screen (May 2011), http://killscreendaily.com/articles/visual-games-photorealism-crisis.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 8" name="FNconcurrence1.8" href="#FRconcurrence1.8">8</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;To see brief video excerpts from games with highly realistic graphics, see Spike TV Video Game Awards 2010--Game of the Year Nominees, GameTrailers.com (Dec. 10, 2010), http://www.gametrailers.com/ video/game-of-spike-tv-vga/707755?type=flv.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 9" name="FNconcurrence1.9" href="#FRconcurrence1.9">9</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Selleck, Sony PS3 Launching 50 3D-Capable Video Games in the Near Future, SlashGear (Nov. 23, 2010), http://www.slashgear.com/ sony-ps3-launching-50-3d-capable-video-games-in-the-near-future-23115866; Sofge, Why 3D Doesn't Work for TV, But Is Great for Gaming, Popular Mechanics (Mar. 11, 2010), http://www.popularmechanics.com/ technology/digital/gaming/4342437.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 10" name="FNconcurrence1.10" href="#FRconcurrence1.10">10</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;T. Chatfield, Fun Inc.: Why Games are the Twenty-first Century's Most Serious Business 211 (2010) (predicting that "[w]e can expect ... physical feedback and motion detection as standard in every gaming device in the near future"); J. Blascovich &amp; J. Bailenson, Infinite Reality: Avatars, Eternal Life, New Worlds, and the Dawn of the Virtual Revolution 2 (2011) ("Technological developments powering virtual worlds are accelerating, ensuring that virtual experiences will become more <em>immersive</em> by providing sensory information that makes people feel they are 'inside' virtual worlds" (emphasis in the original)).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 11" name="FNconcurrence1.11" href="#FRconcurrence1.11">11</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Topolsky, The Mindwire V5 Turns Gaming into Pure Electroshock Torture, Engadget (Mar. 9, 2008), http://www.engadget.com/ 2008&nbsp;/&nbsp;03&nbsp;/&nbsp;09&nbsp;/&nbsp;the&nbsp;-&nbsp;mindwire&nbsp;-&nbsp;v5&nbsp;-&nbsp;turns&nbsp;-&nbsp;gaming&nbsp;-&nbsp;into&nbsp;-&nbsp;pure-electroshock-torture; Greenemeier, Video Game Vest Simulates Sensation of Being Capped, Scientific American (Oct. 25, 2007), http://www.scientificamerican.com/ article.cfm?id=video-game-vest-simulates.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 12" name="FNconcurrence1.12" href="#FRconcurrence1.12">12</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Schiesel, A Real Threat Now Faces the Nintendo Wii, N.&nbsp;Y. Times, Dec. 3, 2010, p. F7 (describing how leading developers of video-game consoles are competing to deliver gesture-controlled gaming devices).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 13" name="FNconcurrence1.13" href="#FRconcurrence1.13">13</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;For a sample of violent video games, see Wilson, The 10 Most Violent Video Games of All Time, PCMag.com (Feb. 10, 2011), http://www.pcmag.com/article2/0,2817,2379959,00.asp.  To see brief video excerpts from violent games, see Chomik, Top 10: Most Violent Video Games, AskMen.com, http://www.askmen.com/top_10/videogame/ top-10-most-violent-video-games.html; Sayed, 15 Most Violent Video Games That Made You Puke, Gamingbolt (May 2, 2010), http:// gamingbolt.com/15-most-violent-video-games-that-made-you-puke.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 14" name="FNconcurrence1.14" href="#FRconcurrence1.14">14</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Webley, "School Shooter" Video Game to Reenact Columbine, Virginia Tech Killings, Time (Apr. 20, 2011), http://newsfeed.time.com/ 2011&nbsp;/&nbsp;04&nbsp;/&nbsp;20&nbsp;/&nbsp;school&nbsp;-&nbsp;shooter&nbsp;-&nbsp;video&nbsp;-&nbsp;game&nbsp;-&nbsp;reenacts-columbine-virginia-tech-killings.  After a Web site that made School Shooter available for download removed it in response to mounting criticism, the developer stated that it may make the game available on its own Web site.  Inside the Sick Site of a School Shooter Mod (Mar. 26, 2011), http://ssnat.com.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 15" name="FNconcurrence1.15" href="#FRconcurrence1.15">15</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Lah, "RapeLay" Video Game Goes Viral Amid Outrage, CNN (Mar. 30, 2010), http://articles.cnn.com/2010-03-30/world/japan.video. game.rape_1_game-teenage-girl-japanese-government?_s=PM:WORLD.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 16" name="FNconcurrence1.16" href="#FRconcurrence1.16">16</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Graham, Custer May be Shot Down Again in a Battle of the Sexes Over X-Rated Video Games, People, Nov. 15, 1982, pp. 110, 115.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 17" name="FNconcurrence1.17" href="#FRconcurrence1.17">17</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Scheeres, Games Elevate Hate to Next Level, Wired (Feb. 20, 2002), http://www.wired.com/culture/lifestyle/news/2002/02/50523.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 18" name="FNconcurrence1.18" href="#FRconcurrence1.18">18</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Thompson, A View to a Kill: JFK Reloaded is Just Plain Creepy, Slate (Nov. 22, 2004), http://www.slate.com/id/2110034.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 19" name="FNconcurrence1.19" href="#FRconcurrence1.19">19</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;As the Court notes, there are a few children's books that ask young readers to step into the shoes of a character and to make choices that take the stories along one of a very limited number of possible lines.  See <em>ante</em>, at 10.  But the very nature of the print medium makes it impossible for a book to offer anything like the same number of choices as those provided by a video game.</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3><br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNdissent1.1" href="#FRdissent1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Alito</small></span> concludes that the law is too vague to satisfy due process, but neither the District Court nor the Court of Appeals addressed that question.  <em>Ante</em>, at 2-9 (opinion concurring in judgment).<em>  </em>As we have often said, this Court is "one of final review, 'not of first view.'&nbsp;"  <em>FCC</em> v. <em>Fox Television Stations, Inc.</em>, 556 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2009) (slip op., at 25) (quoting <em>Cutter</em> v. <em>Wilkinson</em>, 544 U.&nbsp;S. 709, 718, n.&nbsp;7 (2005)).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNdissent1.2" href="#FRdissent1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The majority responds that "it does not follow" from the historical evidence "that the state has the power to prevent children from hearing ... anything <em>without their parents' prior consent</em>."  <em>Ante</em>, at 7, n.&nbsp;3.  Such a conclusion, the majority asserts, would lead to laws that, in its view, would be undesirable and "obviously" unconstitutional.  <em>Ibid.</em> </p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The majority's circular argument misses the point.  The question is not whether certain laws might make sense to judges or legislators today, but rather what the public likely understood "the freedom of speech" to mean when the First Amendment was adopted.  See <em>District of Columbia</em> v. <em>Heller</em>, 554 U.&nbsp;S. 570, 634-635 (2008).  I believe it is clear that the founding public would not have understood "the freedom of speech" to include speech to minor children bypassing their parents.  It follows that the First Amendment imposes no restriction on state regulation of such speech.  To note that there may not be "precedent for [such] state control," <em>ante</em>, at 8, n.&nbsp;3, "is not to establish that [there] is a constitutional right," <em>McIntyre</em> v. <em>Ohio Elections Comm'n</em>, 514 U.&nbsp;S. 334, 373 (1995) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Scalia, J.</small></span>, dissenting).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNdissent1.3" href="#FRdissent1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Whether the statute would survive an as-applied challenge in the unusual case of an emancipated minor is a question for another day.  To decide this case, it is enough that the statute is not unconstitutional on its face.</p>
 
        <p></p>
        <p></p>

_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory = function(nameString, href) {
  // TODO remove this once the correct PA is being used for the lid
  if( _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context') && _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa ) {
    nameString = nameString.replace(/pa:[0-9]+/, "pa:"+_satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa);  
  }
  _satellite.logger.debug('_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory nameString: '+nameString);

  // sample name: "&lid=clk:fs/prod:dts/cid:5088127/msa:259/pa:1313/sub:3107462/pos:1"
  var s = nameString;
  s = s.substr(5);
  var lid = s;
  s = s.replaceAll(':','":"');
  s = s.replaceAll('/','","');
  s = '{"'+s+'"}'
  var adInfo = JSON.parse(s);
  adInfo.lid = lid;
  adInfo.paid = (typeof adInfo.sub != 'undefined');
  adInfo.adId = (adInfo.paid ? adInfo.sub : adInfo.cid);
  var map = {
    'ph':'phone',
    'fs':'website',
    'pv':'profile',
    'cu':'contact',
    'vr':'review-read',
    'wr':'review-write',
    'sl':'super-lawyers'
  };  
  adInfo.type = map[adInfo.clk];   
  adInfo.transactionId = _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id')+'-'+adInfo.adId+'-'+(new Date().getTime());
  
  if( href && adInfo.type == 'phone' ) {
    adInfo.detail = href.replace(/[^\d]/g,'');
    if( adInfo.detail.length > 10 ) {
      adInfo.detail = adInfo.detail.substr(adInfo.detail.length-10);
    }
  }  
  
  return adInfo;
}

_satellite.ads.trackAdEngagement = function(adInfo) {
  if( typeof adInfo.adId == 'undefined' ) {
    return false;
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads = {};
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements = [];
  }
  for( var i=0; i < FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.length; i++ ) {
    if( FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].type == adInfo.type && FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].adId == adInfo.adId ) {
      return true;
    }
  }

  FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.push(adInfo);
  return false;
}

  }

function setTrackerCookie(name, value)
{
  var hostParts = location.host.split('.').reverse();
  var cookieDomain = '.' + hostParts[1] + '.' + hostParts[0];
  document.cookie = name + '=' + value + ';domain=' + cookieDomain + ';path=/';
}

  try {

    _satellite.trackSearchSource = function(type){
      let toStore = type;
      if(type.indexOf("Top-Nav")>-1 || type.indexOf("Next Steps")>-1 ){
        const pageType = _satellite.getVar("page_page_type");
        if(pageType == "article") toStore += " Article";
        if(pageType == "index") toStore += " Index";
      }
      var practiceCode = _satellite.getVar("practice_code");
      if (practiceCode) {
        toStore+= "|"+practiceCode;
      }
      _satellite.logger.info('s_dsource',toStore);
      _satellite.writeCrossSubdomainCookie("s_dsource", toStore);
    };

    _satellite.slowDown = function(e, goTo){
      e.preventDefault(); // prevent default anchor behavior
      setTimeout(function() {
        window.location = goTo;
      }, 300);
    }
    
    //CTA Search - enter & click
    const nextStepsForm = document.querySelector('.nextSteps form');
    _satellite.logger.info('nextStepsForm',nextStepsForm);
    if (nextStepsForm) {
          nextStepsForm.addEventListener('submit', function(e) {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Next Steps");
        });
    }


    //Top Nav & Find a Lawyer Page - Enter
    const searchComponents = document.querySelectorAll('#search-component,.hero-content form, .search-secondary form');
    _satellite.logger.info('searchComponents',searchComponents);
    searchComponents.forEach((component) => {
      component.addEventListener('keypress', function(e) {
        if (e.key === 'Enter') {
          if (location.host + location.pathname == "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
            _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
            _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
          } else {
              _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
              _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
          }
        }
      });
    })
    

    //Top Nav & Find a Lawyer Page - click
    const submitButtons = document.querySelectorAll('submit-button, .hero-content input[type="submit"], .search-secondary input[type="submit"]')
    submitButtons.forEach((button) => {
      _satellite.logger.info('button',button);
      button.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        if (location.host + location.pathname === "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
        } else {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
        }
      });
    })

    //Homepage search
    if (location.host + location.pathname === "www.findlaw.com/") {
      const forms = document.querySelectorAll("form[action*='stdSearch_process.jsp']");
      _satellite.logger.info('forms',forms);
      forms.forEach((form) => {
        _satellite.logger.info('form',form);
        form.addEventListener('submit', function() {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Home");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Home');
        });
      });
    }

    //CTA
    const links = document.querySelectorAll('a[href*="fli="], .articleHeading p a[href*="lawyers.findlaw.com"]');
    _satellite.logger.info('links',links);

      link.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        const goTo = link.getAttribute('href');
        _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
        let source = link.dataset.searchLocation || '';
        if (!source) {
          if (link.closest('.cta-public')) {
            source = 'CTA: Banner';
          } else {
            source = 'CTA: Inline Link';
          }
          _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
          _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
        }
      });
    });

    //Right Rail
    const sidebarLawyerSearch = document.querySelector('.sidebarLawyerSearch');
    _satellite.logger.info('sidebarLawyerSearch',sidebarLawyerSearch);
    if (sidebarLawyerSearch) {
          sidebarLawyerSearch.addEventListener('click', function(event) {
            if (event.target.getAttribute('href') && event.target.getAttribute('href').includes('lawyers.findlaw.com')) {
              const goTo = event.target.getAttribute('href');
              _satellite.slowDown(event, goTo);
              _satellite.trackSearchSource("Link Module: Right Rail");
            }
          });
    }

    //Browse
    if (location.host + location.pathname == "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
      const lawyerLinks = document.querySelectorAll('a[href*="/lawyer"]');
      _satellite.logger.info('lawyerLinks',lawyerLinks);
      lawyersLinks.forEach((link) => {
        link.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
          const goTo = this.getAttribute('href');
          _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
          let source = "";
          if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/statepractice/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Popular Cities";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/state/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: State";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/statecounty/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Top Counties";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/practice/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Legal Issue";
          }
          _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
          _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
        });
      });
    }
    
    //data-search-location
    const linksWithSearchLocation = document.querySelectorAll('[data-search-location] a, a[data-search-location]');
    _satellite.logger.info('linksWithSearchLocation',linksWithSearchLocation);
    linksWithSearchLocation.forEach((link) => {
      link.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        var goTo = this.getAttribute('href');
        _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
        var source = e.currentTarget.dataset.searchLocation || e.currentTarget.dataset.closest("[data-search-location]").dataset.searchLocation;
        _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
        _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
      });
    });

  } catch (exp) {
    _satellite.logger.log("Issue with 'Search Source' Rule");
  }
  
});
